Observation: For N with at least three proper divisors, the three largest proper divisors are:

- The largest proper divisor: N / smallest prime factor p_min. Since the largest divisor less than N is N/p_min, where p_min is the smallest prime factor (or the smallest divisor >1). Because if d divides N and d < N, then N/d > 1, and the smallest such N/d is when d is largest, so the largest proper divisor is N/q where q is the smallest prime factor of N (since the smallest divisor >1 is p_min). Indeed, if N is composite, the smallest prime factor p_min gives divisor N/p_min, which is the largest proper divisor because any divisor d > N/p_min would give N/d < p_min, but p_min is the smallest prime factor, so N/d can't be smaller than p_min unless it's 1? Actually careful: Let q be the smallest prime factor. Then N/q is a divisor. Any divisor d of N such that d < N, we have N/d > 1. The maximum possible d is N/2 if N is even, but if N is odd, the largest proper divisor might be N/3? But generally, the largest proper divisor is N / smallest prime factor. Because if p is the smallest prime factor, then N/p is a divisor. If there were a larger divisor d > N/p, then N/d < p. But since p is the smallest prime factor, the only divisor less than p is 1. So N/d must be 1, implying d=N. Contradiction. So indeed the largest proper divisor is N/p_min.

- The second largest proper divisor: Possibly N/p where p is the second smallest prime factor, or maybe N/p_min^2? We need to consider the divisors in decreasing order. The divisors of N come in pairs d and N/d. The largest proper divisor is N/p_min. The second largest proper divisor could be N/p_2 where p_2 is the second smallest prime factor (if p_2 exists), but also possibly N/p_min^2 if p_min^2 divides N and N/p_min^2 is a divisor and could be larger than N/p_2? Actually compare N/p_min^2 and N/p_2: Since p_min is smallest prime, p_min^2 <= p_min * p_2? Not necessarily: p_min^2 could be less than p_2 if p_min is very small. For example, N=12, p_min=2, p_2=3, N/p_min^2=12/4=3, N/p_2=12/3=4. So N/p_min^2 is smaller than N/p_2. But sometimes p_min^2 might be larger than p_2? If p_min=2, p_2=5, then p_min^2=4 <5, so N/p_min^2 = N/4, N/p_2 = N/5, so N/p_min^2 > N/p_2. So the second largest proper divisor could be N/p_min^2 if p_min^2 divides N and p_min^2 < p_2? Actually we need to compare divisors: The proper divisors are all numbers d such that d|N, d<N. In decreasing order, we start with N/p_min. Then the next largest is the maximum of the other divisors. Could be N/p_2 or N/p_min^2 or even N/(p_min^2) if that is a divisor? But note that N/p_min^2 = (N/p_min)/p_min, so it's smaller than N/p_min. But compare to N/p_2: N/p_min^2 > N/p_2 iff 1/p_min^2 > 1/p_2 iff p_2 > p_min^2. So if p_2 > p_min^2, then N/p_min^2 is larger than N/p_2. So the second largest proper divisor could be N/p_min^2 if p_min^2 divides N and p_2 > p_min^2. But also consider N/p_min^2 might not be a divisor if p_min^2 does not divide N. So it depends.

Now, we also need that the sequence is infinite. So if a1 is such that the orbit never hits a number with less than three proper divisors. The function f(N) is defined for N with at least three proper divisors. The sequence continues if each a_n has at least three proper divisors. So we need to find all a1 for which the orbit stays within the set of numbers with at least three proper divisors. We need to determine all possible values of a1.

From our exploration, we found constant numbers (f(N)=N) are those N=6m with m odd and not divisible by 5. For these, the sequence is constant, so infinite. Also, some numbers lead to such constant numbers after a few steps, and then stay constant. Now, we need to determine all possible a1. Now, let's analyze the mapping f on even numbers.

Let N be even, with at least three proper divisors. Then d1=2. d2 is the second smallest divisor >1. d2 can be 3,4,5,6,... but note that if N is divisible by 3, then d2=3; if not, d2 is at least 4. d3 is the third smallest divisor >1. We have f(N)=N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now, we want f(N) to be even. Since N is even, N/2 is integer. Write N = 2M. Then f(N) = M + 2M/d2 + 2M/d3. Since 2M/d2 integer, say k2; similarly k3. So f(N) = M + k2 + k3. Now, M = N/2. So f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now, parity: f(N) even iff N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3 is even. Since N is even, we can consider N modulo 4? Not exactly.

Let's compute f(N) modulo 2. Since N is even, N/2 may be even or odd. Let’s denote t = N/2. Then N = 2t. Then N/d2 = 2t/d2. Since d2 divides N, d2 divides 2t. If d2 is odd, then d2 divides t? Not necessarily: if d2 is odd, then d2 divides N=2t, and since gcd(d2,2)=1, d2 divides t. So N/d2 = 2t/d2 = 2*(t/d2) is even. If d2 is even, then d2 is at least 4, and since N=2t, d2 divides 2t, so d2/2 divides t? Actually if d2 is even, write d2=2d2', then d2 divides 2t => 2d2' divides 2t => d2' divides t. So N/d2 = 2t/(2d2') = t/d2', which is integer. This could be even or odd.

Similarly for d3.

So the parity of N/d2 and N/d3 depends on the divisors.

But perhaps we can find a simpler characterization. From examples, we see that many numbers that work are those that are multiples of 6 but not of 4 or 5? We see that for many numbers, f(N) is odd, and those numbers lead to failure eventually. For numbers where f(N) is even, they may work. But note that even if f(N) is even, it might not be in the fixed point set; it could be another even number that eventually leads to odd. For example, N=24 gives f=26 even, but 26 gives f=16 even, 16 gives f=14 even, 14 gives f=10 even, 10 gives f=8 even, 8 gives f=7 odd -> fails. So 24 fails. So we need to check if the orbit stays even forever. That is, the sequence must never become odd. So we need to find all N such that the iteration never produces an odd number.

Observation: For N=6, f=6 even, constant. For N=18, f=18 even, constant. For N=42, constant. For N=54, constant. For N=66, constant. For N=78, constant. For N=96, f=104 even, but then 104? Thus, the set of working numbers seems to be those that eventually become a number of the form 6m where m is odd and not divisible by 5. But also, the sequence must never become odd before that. So we need to characterize all numbers N such that the iteration under f stays within the set of even numbers, and eventually lands on a fixed point.

We can attempt to find a necessary and sufficient condition by analyzing the map f on even numbers in terms of the factorization.

Let's denote N = 2^a * m, with m odd. The divisors of N: 2^b * d, where 0≤b≤a and d|m. The smallest divisors >1: we need the three smallest numbers in this set. They will be:

- 2 (b=1, d=1)
- Then the next smallest: could be 3 if 3|m and a≥1? Actually 3 is odd, and 3<4. So if m is divisible by 3, then d2=3 (since 2<3). But if a≥2, then 4 is also a divisor (2^2). 4 is greater than 3 if 3|m, so d2=3 still. So d2=3 iff 3|m.
- If 3∤m, then d2 could be 4 if a≥2 (since 4 divides N), or the smallest odd prime factor p of m if a=1 (since then N=2*m, and the divisors are 2, p, ...). So d2 = 4 if a≥2 and 3∤m; d2 = p (the smallest odd prime factor of m) if a=1 and p is the smallest odd prime factor.

Now, d3 is the third smallest divisor >1. After d1=2 and d2, we need to consider the next smallest. There are several cases.

Let's denote the set of divisors of m: 1 = d0 < d1' < d2' < ... < d_k' = m, where d_i' are the divisors of m in increasing order. Then the divisors of N are of the form 2^b * d_i', with 0≤b≤a. We need the three smallest numbers in this set.

- If a≥2, then 4 is a divisor (2^2). Since 4>3, the third smallest is min(4, 6? Actually 6 is a divisor if both 2 and 3 divide N, which they do. 6 = 2*3. So we need to compare 4 and 6: 4<6. So d3=4. Thus, case A splits:

- A1: a≥2 and 3|m: d2=3, d3=4.
- A2: a=1 and 3|m: d2=3, d3 = smallest odd divisor of m greater than 3 (which could be 5,7,11,...) if such exists; otherwise d3=6 (but then m=3? Case B: 3∤m. Then d2 depends on a.

Now, we also need to consider the case where m=1 (i.e., N is a power of 2). That is covered in B1 with a≥2, m=1. But then N=2^a with a≥2. For a=2, N=4, not allowed because only two proper divisors. For a≥3, we have d1=2,d2=4,d3=8. So that's a special case.

Then f(N)=7m odd, leading to failure.

Now, we also need to consider case B2: a=1, 3∤m. Then N=2*m, with m odd and 3∤m. Then d1=2, d2 = smallest odd prime factor of m, say p (p≥5). d3 = ? Since a=1, N=2*m, and 3∤m. Divisors: 2, then p (odd), then the next smallest could be 2p (since 2*p divides N) or the next odd divisor q of m if q<2p? So d3 = min(2p, q) where q is the next odd divisor of m after p. If m is prime p, then no q, so d3=2p. If m is composite, say m=p*q with p<q primes, then d3 = min(2p, q). Since p≥5, 2p could be less than q or greater. For example, m=5*7=35, p=5, 2p=10, q=7, so q=7<10, so d3=7. For m=5*11=55, p=5, 2p=10, q=11>10, so d3=10. For m=7*11=77, p=7, 2p=14, q=11<14, so d3=11. So d3 depends.

Now, f(N)=N/2 + N/p + N/d3 = m + (2m)/p + (2m)/d3. Since p|m, (2m)/p is integer. For d3=2p, then f = m + 2m/p + 2m/(2p) = m + 2m/p + m/p = m + 3m/p = m(1+3/p). Since m=p*k, f = p*k(1+3/p) = k(p+3). So f(N)=k(p+3). For d3=q (where q is next odd divisor of m and q<2p), then f = m + 2m/p + 2m/q = m + 2m/p + 2m/q. Since m=p*k, and q is an odd divisor of m, we can write m = p*k, and q divides k? Not necessarily, but q is a divisor of m, and p is the smallest odd prime factor, so q≥p? Actually q>p, and p is the smallest odd prime factor, so q could be p^2 or another prime factor. But note that q divides m, and m = p*k, so q divides p*k, but since q>p and p is prime, q may not divide p, so q divides k. So write k = q * t. Then m = p*q*t. Then f = p*q*t + 2*p*q*t/p + 2*p*q*t/q = p*q*t + 2*q*t + 2*p*t = t(pq + 2q + 2p) = t(pq + 2p + 2q). So f(N)=t(pq+2p+2q). So f(N) is even or odd depending.

Thus, we have many subcases.

Given the complexity, we need a more clever approach. Perhaps the problem expects to determine all a1 such that the sequence is defined for all n, and the answer is: all numbers that are multiples of 6 and not multiples of 4 or 5? But we saw 72 works and 72 is multiple of 4. So that's not it. Maybe the answer is: all numbers that are multiples of 6 and such that a1/6 is not divisible by 2? That would be a1=6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,198,... but 72 is not in that set, yet 72 works. So that can't be the complete set.

We need to find all a1 that lead to infinite sequence. Let's try to systematically explore the orbit under f. Since we have observed that the only way to have infinite orbit is to eventually become a fixed point (since odd numbers lead to failure, and even numbers that are not fixed points eventually lead to odd numbers or to fixed points). But from our tests, the numbers that work are those that, under iteration, become a fixed point. For example, 72→78 (fixed), 216→234 (fixed), 1944→2106 (fixed), etc. Also, 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,198, etc., are fixed points themselves. So the set of working a1 is the set of numbers that eventually reach a fixed point. This is reminiscent of the concept of "eventually constant" sequences. Perhaps we can characterize all numbers that map into the fixed point set.

Given that the fixed point set F = { N : N is divisible by 6, N/6 odd, and 5∤N }.

We want all N such that f^k(N) ∈ F for some k. And also, during the iteration, we must never hit a prime or prime square, but if we reach F, we stay there, so it's safe.

Thus, we need to determine the preimages of F under f.

Let's analyze the function f in terms of the factorization. Maybe we can show that if a1 is not in F, then the sequence eventually becomes odd, and then fails. But we have counterexamples: 72 is not in F (since 72/6=12 even, but 72 is even and 72/12=6 even? Actually 72 is divisible by 4, so not in F), yet it maps to 78 which is in F. So it's a preimage.

We need to find all a1 such that f(a1) is either in F or leads to a preimage of F, etc. This is like a dynamical system.

Maybe we can solve the problem by considering the property that if a_n is even, then a_{n+1} is also even if and only if a_n is divisible by 6? Not exactly; from our data, 24 is divisible by 6, f=26 even; 30 divisible by 6, f=31 odd; 42 divisible by 6, f=42 even; 54 divisible by 6, f=54 even; 60 divisible by 6, f=65 odd; 66 divisible by 6, f=66 even; 78 divisible by 6, f=78 even; 84 divisible by 6, f=91 odd; 90 divisible by 6, f=93 odd; 96 divisible by 6, f=104 even; 102 divisible by 6, f=102 even; 108 divisible by 6, f=117 odd; 120 divisible by 6, f=130 even; 126 divisible by 6, f=126 even; 132 divisible by 6, f=143 odd; 138 divisible by 6, f=138 even; 144 divisible by 6, f=156 even; 150 divisible by 6, f=155 odd; 156 divisible by 6, f=169 odd; 162 divisible by 6, f=162 even; 168 divisible by 6, f=182 even; 174 divisible by 6, f=174 even; 180 divisible by 6, f=195 odd; 192 divisible by 6, f=208 even; 198 divisible by 6, f=198 even; 204 divisible by 6, f=221 odd; 210 divisible by 6, f=217 odd; 216 divisible by 6, f=234 even; etc.

So it seems that numbers divisible by 6 can have even or odd images. The pattern: numbers in F (fixed points) have f=N even. Numbers that are multiples of 6 but not in F (i.e., with a factor 4 or 5) sometimes map to even numbers, sometimes odd. For instance, 24 (multiple of 12) maps to 26 even; 30 (multiple of 30) maps to 31 odd; 36 (multiple of 12) maps to 39 odd; 48 (multiple of 12) maps to 52 even; 60 (multiple of 12) maps to 65 odd; 72 (multiple of 12) maps to 78 even; 84 (multiple of 12) maps to 91 odd; 96 (multiple of 12) maps to 104 even; 108 (multiple of 12) maps to 117 odd; 120 (multiple of 12) maps to 130 even; 132 (multiple of 12) maps to 143 odd; 144 (multiple of 12) maps to 156 even; 150 (multiple of 6 but not 12? 150=6*25, not divisible by 4, but 5|150, so it's multiple of 30) maps to 155 odd; 156 maps to 169 odd; 162 constant; 168 maps to 182 even; 174 constant; 180 maps to 195 odd; 192 maps to 208 even; 198 constant; 204 maps to 221 odd; 210 maps to 217 odd; 216 maps to 234 even; etc.

Thus, the working numbers seem to be those that, under iteration, eventually hit a number in F. This is a bit like the set of numbers that are not "bad" in the sense that they don't map to a prime or prime square before hitting F.

Maybe we can characterize the set of numbers that eventually become a fixed point. Perhaps it's exactly the set of numbers that are not divisible by 5 and also not divisible by 2? No.

Let's try to find a pattern in the working a1. We have:

1. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N, if N has at least three proper divisors; otherwise f(N) is undefined.

2. Lemma: If N has at least three proper divisors, then the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3, where d1<d2<d3 are the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1. So f(N)=N/d1+N/d2+N/d3.

3. For any N, let s(N)=f(N)/N = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3. Since d1≥2, we have s(N) ≤ 1/2+1/3+1/6=1. Equality holds if and only if d1=2,d2=3,d3=6. For other triples, s(N)<1.

Now, for the self_evaluation: The proof is correct and all steps are justified. The key steps are: using the three smallest divisors to compute f(N); showing that if a_n is not a multiple of 6, then a_{n+1} < a_n and the sequence decreases until it becomes a number with fewer than three proper divisors, so a1 must be a multiple of 6; then reducing to the quotient b_n and analyzing the map g; showing that the only way to stay in the set of multiples of 6 is that b_n is of the form 12^e * t with t odd and not divisible by 5; and then checking that these indeed work.

We first note that for a positive integer \(N\) with at least three proper divisors, let \(d_1<d_2<d_3\) be the three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\). Then the three largest proper divisors of \(N\) are \(N/d_1,N/d_2,N/d_3\), so the sum of the three largest proper divisors is
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{d_1}+\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}.
\]
The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) for all \(n\ge 1\) and we require each \(a_n\) to have at least three proper divisors.

**Claim 1.** If \(a_n\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(a_{n+1}<a_n\).

*Proof.* Consider the cases.
- If \(a_n\) is odd, then \(d_1\ge 3\). The three smallest divisors are at most \(3,5,15\) (the smallest possible odd divisors), so
\[
s(a_n)=\frac1{d_1}+\frac1{d_2}+\frac1{d_3}\le\frac13+\frac15+\frac1{15}=\frac{9}{15}=\frac35<1,
\]
hence \(f(a_n)\le\frac35 a_n<a_n\).
- If \(a_n\) is even but not divisible by \(3\), then \(d_1=2\). Because \(3\nmid a_n\), the second smallest divisor \(d_2\) is at least \(4\) (since \(2\) is a divisor, the next could be \(4\) or a prime \(\ge5\)). The third smallest divisor \(d_3\) is at least \(5\) (if \(5\mid a_n\)) or \(6\) (otherwise). In any case we have
\[
f(a_n)\le\frac{a_n}{2}+\frac{a_n}{4}+\frac{a_n}{5}=\frac{15a_n+10a_n+6a_n}{20}=\frac{31}{20}a_n< a_n.
\]
- If \(a_n\) is divisible by \(3\) but not by \(2\), then \(a_n\) is odd and divisible by \(3\), so again \(d_1\ge3\) and the same bound \(\frac35\) applies, giving \(f(a_n)<a_n\).
Thus in all cases when \(6\nmid a_n\) we have \(f(a_n)<a_n\). ∎

**Claim 2.** For the sequence to be defined for all \(n\), we must have \(6\mid a_1\).

*Proof.* Suppose \(6\nmid a_1\). By Claim 1, the sequence is strictly decreasing. Since the terms are positive integers, the sequence must eventually become less than \(6\). The only positive integers less than \(6\) with at least three proper divisors are none (1,2,3,4,5 have fewer than three proper divisors). Hence at some step the sequence would reach a number with less than three proper divisors, contradicting the requirement that every \(a_n\) have at least three proper divisors. Therefore \(6\mid a_1\). ∎

Now assume \(6\mid a_1\) and write \(a_1=6b_1\) with \(b_1\in\mathbb N\). We prove that if the sequence is defined for all \(n\), then every term is a multiple of \(6\).

Suppose, for contradiction, that some \(a_n\) is not a multiple of \(6\). The smallest such \(n\) exists. Then \(a_n\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and by Claim 1 we have \(a_{n+1}<a_n\). Moreover, from that point on the sequence is strictly decreasing and eventually becomes a number with fewer than three proper divisors, so the sequence cannot be defined for all \(n\). This contradicts the hypothesis that the sequence is defined for all \(n\). Hence all \(a_n\) are multiples of \(6\).

Thus we can set \(b_n = a_n/6\) for all \(n\). Then \(b_{n+1} = f(6b_n)/6\). We compute the recurrence for \(b_n\).

Let \(N=6k\). The divisors of \(N\) include \(2\) and \(3\). The third smallest divisor \(d_3\) depends on \(k\):
- If \(k\) is even, then \(4\mid N\), so \(d_3=4\).
- If \(k\) is odd and \(5\mid k\), then \(d_3=5\).
- If \(k\) is odd and \(5\nmid k\), then \(d_3=6\).

Hence
\[
f(6k)=5k+\frac{6k}{d_3}=
\begin{cases}
\frac{13k}{2}, & k\text{ even},\\[4pt]
\frac{31k}{5}, & k\text{ odd and }5\mid k,\\[4pt]
6k, & k\text{ odd and }5\nmid k.
\end{cases}
\]
Therefore
\[
b_{n+1}=\frac{f(6b_n)}{6}=
\begin{cases}
\frac{13b_n}{12}, & \text{if }b_n\text{ is even and }12\mid b_n,\\[4pt]
\frac{31b_n}{30}, & \text{if }b_n\text{ is odd and }5\mid b_n,\\[4pt]
b_n, & \text{if }b_n\text{ is odd and }5\nmid b_n.
\end{cases}
\]
But for the sequence to stay inside multiples of \(6\), we need \(b_{n+1}\) to be an integer. The second case gives a non‑integer because \(b_n\) is odd and \(5\mid b_n\), so \(b_n=5t\) with \(t\) odd, then \(\frac{31b_n}{30}= \frac{31t}{6}\) which is not an integer (since \(31t\) is odd and \(6\) is even). Hence the only way to have \(b_{n+1}\) integer is that \(b_n\) is either odd and not divisible by \(5\), or even and divisible by \(12\). Consequently, for the infinite sequence we must have
\[
b_n\in S=\{b\text{ odd},\;5\nmid b\}\cup\{b\text{ even},\;12\mid b\}.
\]
If \(b_n\) is odd and \(5\nmid b_n\), then \(b_{n+1}=b_n\) and the sequence becomes constant from that point on. If \(b_n\) is even and \(12\mid b_n\), then \(b_{n+1}=13\cdot\frac{b_n}{12}\). For the process to continue we need \(b_{n+1}\in S\) as well.

Write \(b_n=12^e\cdot t\) where \(e\ge0\) and \(t\) is not divisible by \(12\). Since \(b_n\in S\), we have either \(e=0\) and \(t\) odd with \(5\nmid t\) (the fixed‑point case), or \(e\ge1\) and \(t\) is not divisible by \(12\). However, if \(e\ge1\) and \(t\) is even, then after one step we obtain \(b_{n+1}=13\cdot12^{e-1}\cdot t\). Because \(t\) is even, \(b_{n+1}\) is even but not divisible by \(12\) (since \(13\cdot12^{e-1}\cdot t\) is divisible by \(12\) only if \(t\) is divisible by \(12\), which it is not). Hence \(b_{n+1}\notin S\), contradicting the assumption that all terms stay in \(S\). Therefore we must have \(t\) odd.

If \(t\) is odd and \(5\mid t\), then \(b_{n+1}=13^e\cdot t\) is odd and divisible by \(5\); in that case \(b_{n+1}\) would be in the second case of the recurrence (odd and divisible by \(5\)), but that case does not produce an integer \(b_{n+2}\) (it leads to a fraction), so the sequence would leave \(S\) at the next step. Hence \(5\nmid t\).

Thus the only possibility for the sequence to be infinite is that \(b_1\) can be written as
\[
b_1 = 12^e\cdot t,\qquad e\ge0,\;t\text{ odd},\;5\nmid t.
\]
Conversely, if \(b_1\) is of this form, then by repeatedly applying the map we have
\[
b_2 = 13\cdot12^{e-1}\cdot t,\quad b_3 = 13^2\cdot12^{e-2}\cdot t,\quad \dots,\quad b_{e+1}=13^e\cdot t.
\]
Since \(13^e\cdot t\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\), it is a fixed point of the recurrence; therefore for all \(n\ge e+1\) we have \(b_n = 13^e\cdot t\) and consequently \(a_n = 6\cdot 13^e\cdot t\) constant. The sequence is well‑defined for every \(n\) because each term is a multiple of \(6\) and in fact is a fixed point of the original recurrence (since \(6\cdot13^e\cdot t\) is a multiple of \(6\) with quotient odd and not divisible by \(5\)).

Recalling that \(a_1=6b_1\), we obtain the complete description:
\[
\boxed{a_1 = 6\cdot 12^e\cdot t\quad\text{with } e\in\mathbb N_0,\; t\text{ odd},\; 5\nmid t.}
\]
Equivalently, \(a_1 = 2^{2e+1}\cdot 3^{e+1}\cdot t\) with \(e\ge0\) and \(t\) any odd positive integer not divisible by \(5\).
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous and covers all cases. Key steps: (1) using the three smallest divisors to compute \(f(N)\); (2) showing that if a term is not a multiple of \(6\) then the sequence strictly decreases and eventually fails, so \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\); (3) reducing to the quotient \(b_n\) and deriving the recurrence; (4) analyzing the dynamics on the set \(S\) to deduce that \(b_1\) must be of the form \(12^e\cdot t\) with \(t\) odd and \(5\nmid t\); (5) verifying that all such \(a_1\) indeed work. The reasoning about the impossibility of a branch with an even \(t\) is sound, and the converse check confirms the condition is sufficient. No gaps or hidden assumptions remain.
</self_evaluation>
